<p><b>Thomas Jefferson</b> (April 13 <small>[<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> April 2]</small>&#160;1743&#160;– July 4, 1826) was an American <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father</a>, principal author of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> (1776), and third <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1801–1809). A proponent of democracy, Jefferson embraced the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> and individual rights. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, he represented Virginia in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> and later served as a wartime Governor of Virginia (1779–1781). In May 1785, he became the United States Minister to France and subsequently the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a> from 1790–1793, serving under President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. Jefferson and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> organized the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> to oppose the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> in the formation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a>. He was elected to the office of <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> in 1796, the second in history, serving in the administration of President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>. Jefferson and Madison secretly wrote the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a> in 1798–1799, documents intended to nullify the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>; the resolutions were passed by the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist-controlled</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>.</p>
<p>As a Democratic-Republican, he was elected the third President of the United States in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">election of 1800</a>. After peace negotiations with France, his administration reduced military forces. Three years later he waged a brief naval campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a> states in North Africa to defend American shipping. During his presidency, the United States purchased the vast western <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Territory</a> from Napoleonic France (1803), and sent out the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a> (1804–1806) to explore the new West. His second term was beset with difficulties at home, including the failed treason trial of former Vice President <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. Also, American foreign trade was diminished when Jefferson signed and implemented the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, in response to British threats to U.S. shipping. In 1803, Jefferson initiated a controversial process of Indian tribe removal to the newly organized <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a> west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. In 1807, he signed into law the intensely debated <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a> that banned slave importation into the United States.</p>
<p>A champion of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, Jefferson, was diversely talented in the arts, sciences, agriculture and politics. He was a proven architect in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical tradition</a>, and designed his home <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_State_Capitol" title="Virginia State Capitol">Virginia State Capitol</a> and other important buildings. His keen interest in religion and philosophy also earned him the presidency of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>. Besides English, he was well versed in <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language" class="mw-redirect">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.</p>
<p>He founded the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> in his retirement from public office. Although ineffectual as an orator, Jefferson was a skilled writer and corresponded with many influential people in America and Europe. Most historians believe that after the death of his wife Martha in 1782, he had a long-term relationship, possibly non-consensual, with his female slave <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a>, and fathered at least some of her children.</p>
<p>In the aggregate, Jefferson is ranked by historians as the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">fifth most successful President</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson">Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG/220px-Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG/330px-Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG/440px-Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tuckahoe_plantation.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tuckahoe Plantation, Jefferson's childhood home for 7 years</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was born the third of ten children, on April 13, 1743 (April 2, 1743 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">OS</a>) at the family home in <a href="/wiki/Shadwell_(Virginia)" title="Shadwell (Virginia)">Shadwell, Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> His father was <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jefferson" title="Peter Jefferson">Peter Jefferson</a>, a planter and surveyor who died when Jefferson was fourteen. His mother was <a href="/wiki/Jane_Randolph_Jefferson" title="Jane Randolph Jefferson">Jane Randolph</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Isham_Randolph_of_Dungeness" title="Isham Randolph of Dungeness">Isham Randolph</a>, a ship's captain and planter. He showed little interest in learning about his ancestry; on his father's side he only knew of the existence of his grandfather.<sup id="cite_ref-Brodie33_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brodie33-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Malone5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone5-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson's earliest memory was being handed to a slave on horseback and carried 50 miles away to their new home which overlooked the <a href="/wiki/Rivanna_River" title="Rivanna River">Rivanna River</a> in current <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a>. His facial appearance resembled that of his father but his slim physique was typical of his mother's family.<sup id="cite_ref-Brodie33_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brodie33-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> He was of English and possible Welsh descent.<sup id="cite_ref-Malone5_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone5-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Peter Jefferson's friend William Randolph died a widower in 1745, having appointed Peter as guardian to manage his <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe_Plantation" title="Tuckahoe Plantation" class="mw-redirect">Tuckahoe Plantation</a> and care for his four children. That year the Jeffersons relocated to Tuckahoe where they lived for the next seven years before returning to Shadwell in 1752. Peter Jefferson died in 1757 and the Jefferson estate was divided between Peter's two sons, Thomas and <a href="/wiki/Randolph_Jefferson" title="Randolph Jefferson">Randolph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Thomas inherited approximately 5,000 acres (2,000&#160;ha; 7.8&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land, including <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>, and between 20 and 40 slaves, and unfettered control of the property at age 21.<sup id="cite_ref-Malone437_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone437-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_education" title="Thomas Jefferson and education">Thomas Jefferson and education</a></div>
<p>Jefferson began his childhood education beside the Randolph children with tutors at Tuckahoe.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1752, he began attending a local school run by a Scottish Presbyterian minister. At age nine, he initiated his study of Latin, Greek, and French; he learned to ride horses, and began nature studies. He was taught from 1758 to 1760 by Reverend <a href="/wiki/James_Maury" title="James Maury">James Maury</a> near <a href="/wiki/Gordonsville,_Virginia" title="Gordonsville, Virginia">Gordonsville, Virginia</a> while boarding with Maury's family and there studied history, science and the classics.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/220px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/330px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/440px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wren Building (rear), College of William &amp; Mary where Jefferson studied</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson entered <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="The College of William &amp; Mary" class="mw-redirect">the College of William &amp; Mary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg,_Virginia" title="Williamsburg, Virginia">Williamsburg, Virginia</a> at age 16, and studied mathematics, metaphysics and philosophy under Professor <a href="/wiki/William_Small" title="William Small">William Small</a>. Small introduced him to the <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">British Empiricists</a> including <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. He also improved his French, Greek and his skill at the violin. Jefferson graduated in 1762, completing his studies in two years. Through Small he made the acquaintance of law professor <a href="/wiki/George_Wythe" title="George Wythe">George Wythe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson <a href="/wiki/Reading_the_law" title="Reading the law" class="mw-redirect">read the law</a> under the tutelage of Wythe to obtain his law license, while working as a <a href="/wiki/Law_clerk" title="Law clerk">law clerk</a> in Wythe's office. He also read a wide variety of English classics and political works.</p>
<p>Jefferson treasured his books, and in 1770 his Shadwell home, including a library of 200 volumes inherited from his father, was destroyed by fire;<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> nevertheless, by 1773 he replenished his library with 1,250 titles, and in 1815 his collection had grown to almost 6,500 volumes, including some inherited from George Wythe.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> He thus accumulated thousands of books for his library at Monticello. After the British burned the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> in 1814, he sold more than 6,000 books to the Library for $23,950. Then realizing he was no longer in possession of such a grand collection, he wrote to <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, "I cannot live without books". He had intended to pay off some of his large debt, yet started buying more books.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage.2C_family_and_Monticello">Marriage, family and Monticello</span></h3>
<p>On January 1, 1772 Jefferson married his third cousin <a href="/wiki/Martha_Wayles_Skelton_Jefferson" title="Martha Wayles Skelton Jefferson" class="mw-redirect">Martha Wayles Skelton</a>, the 23-year-old widow of Bathurst Skelton.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Their marriage took place at the house of Martha's father and was conducted by the Reverend William Coutts.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> She was a frequent hostess for Jefferson, managed the large household and their joyful marriage is considered the happiest period of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Martha read widely, did fine needle work and was an skilled pianist; Jefferson, who was accomplished on the violin and cello, often accompanied her musically.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> It is said that she was attracted to him largely because of their mutual love of music.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> During their ten years of marriage, Martha bore six children: <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Martha</a> "Patsy" (1772–1836); Jane (1774–1775); a son who lived for only a few weeks in 1777; <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jefferson_Eppes" title="Mary Jefferson Eppes">Mary Wayles</a> "Polly" (1778–1804); Lucy Elizabeth (1780–1781); and another Lucy Elizabeth (1782–1785). Only Martha and Mary survived more than a few years.<sup id="cite_ref-Martha_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> After her father John Wayles died in 1773, Martha and her husband inherited 135 slaves, 11,000 acres (4,500&#160;ha; 17&#160;sq&#160;mi) and the debts of his estate. These took Jefferson and other co-executors of the estate years to satisfy, which contributed to his own financial problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/220px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/330px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/440px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's home, Monticello</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Martha later suffered from ill health including diabetes, and frequent childbirth further weakened her. A few months after the birth of her last child, she died on September 6, 1782, at the age of 33 with Jefferson at her bedside. He was so distraught that in the following three weeks he shut himself in his room, where he paced back and forth nearly to the point of exhaustion. Later he often took long rides on secluded roads to mourn for his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-Martha_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halliday48_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday48-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Martha's mother had died young, and as a girl Martha lived with two stepmothers. Shortly before her death, she told Jefferson that she could not bear to have another mother raise her children, and pleaded with him to never to marry again. He made and kept a solemn promise in that regard.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson in 1768 had begun construction of his primary residence Monticello (Italian for "Little Mountain") on a hilltop overlooking a 5,000 acre plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> Construction was done mostly by local masons and carpenters, appreciably assisted by Jefferson's slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> He moved into the South Pavilion in 1770, where his wife Martha joined him in 1772. Turning Monticello into a neoclassical masterpiece in the <a href="/wiki/Palladian_architecture" title="Palladian architecture">Palladian</a> style was his perennial project.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson mastered architecture through self-study, using various books and classical architectural designs of the day. His primary authority was <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Palladio" title="Andrea Palladio">Andrea Palladio</a>'s <i>The Four Books of Architecture</i>, which exposed him to the principles of classical design.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> While serving as Minister to France, Jefferson had the opportunity to survey the foremost classical buildings he had reviewed in his reading, as well the "modern" trends in French architecture then fashionable in Paris. After working as Secretary of State (1790–93), he began rebuilding Monticello based on the concepts he had acquired in Europe. The remodeling continued throughout most of his presidency, the most notable change being the octagonal dome.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawyer_and_House_of_Burgesses">Lawyer and House of Burgesses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/220px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/330px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/440px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="971" data-file-height="763" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
House of Burgesses in Williamsburg, VA where Jefferson served 1769–1775</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was admitted to the Virginia bar in 1767 and then lived with his mother at Shadwell.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> His law practice included clients from his mother's family, the Randolphs.,<sup id="cite_ref-HSR_47_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HSR_47-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> and his work took him throughout the Shenandoah Valley.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The fire at Shadwell had also claimed his legal papers and notes for the coming term of court, and though he was frantic, George Wythe consoled him with a line from Virgil, "Carry on, and preserve yourselves for better times."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to practicing law Jefferson represented <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> as a delegate in the Virginia <a href="/wiki/House_of_Burgesses" title="House of Burgesses">House of Burgesses</a> from 1769 until 1775.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> He proved more willing to reform slavery in his early career than later when he became a more substantial slaveholder. In 1769 he introduced legislation allowing masters to assume full control over the emancipation of slaves, taking the discretion away from the royal Governor and his General Court. Jefferson had persuaded his cousin Richard Bland to spearhead the legislation's passage, but the reaction in the House was strongly negative. He recalled Bland was "treated with the grossest indecorum."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson worked on a number of lawsuits on behalf of freedom-seeking slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> He took the case of Samuel Howell without charging him a fee.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon99_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Howell, of inter-racial grandparents, claimed he should be freed before the statutory age of thirty-one required for emancipation in such a case. Jefferson, invoking <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural Law</a>, argued, "everyone comes into the world with a right to his own person and using it at his own will&#160;... This is what is called personal liberty, and is given him by the author of nature, because it is necessary for his own sustenance." The judge hearing the Howell case abruptly cut him off and ruled against his client. As a consolation, Jefferson gave Howell some money, presumably used to aid his escape shortly thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon99_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson later successfully incorporated the argument into the Declaration of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although earlier discouraged, smallpox inoculation began in 1768–1769 in Norfolk County, Virginia, and precipitated riots. Jefferson defended the riots' victims, including Dr. Archibald Campbell whose house was burned. Jefferson, himself inoculated at age 23, gave up his law practice before the case was resolved, but later served on a General Assembly committee which sought to reduce the 1769 restrictions on the inoculations.<sup id="cite_ref-TJFencyclopedia_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFencyclopedia-40"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a> by the British Parliament in 1774, Jefferson wrote a resolution against the acts calling for <i>The day of Fasting and Prayer</i> in protest. The resolution also called for a boycott of all British goods. These were later expanded into <i><a href="/wiki/A_Summary_View_of_the_Rights_of_British_America" title="A Summary View of the Rights of British America">A Summary View of the Rights of British America</a></i>, in which he expressed his belief that people had the right to <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">govern themselves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career_1775.E2.80.931800">Political career 1775–1800</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declaration_of_Independence">Declaration of Independence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/220px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/330px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/440px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3923" data-file-height="4656" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Declaration of Independence - 1823 facsimile of the engrossed copy</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson served as a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> beginning in 1775 at the outbreak of the American Revolutionary War.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> He sought out John Adams who, along with the latter's cousin Samuel, had emerged as a leader of the Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson and Adams established a permanent friendship which led to Jefferson primary drafting of the Declaration of Independence. Adams supported Jefferson's appointment to the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> formed to write the formal Declaration in furtherance of the <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">Lee Resolution</a> passed by the Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> After discussing the general outline of the document, the committee decided that Jefferson would write the first draft. The committee in general, and Jefferson in particular, thought Adams should write the document, but Adams persuaded the committee to choose Jefferson. Though he was reluctant to take the assignment, he agreed and Adams promised to consult with him upon completion.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Consulting with other committee members over the next seventeen days, Jefferson also drew on his own proposed draft of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Virginia" title="Constitution of Virginia">Virginia Constitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a>'s draft of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Declaration_of_Rights" title="Virginia Declaration of Rights">Virginia Declaration of Rights</a> and other sources.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The other committee members made some changes; most notably, Jefferson had written, "We hold these truths to be sacred and un-deniable&#160;..." which Franklin changed to read, "We hold these truths to be self-evident."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> A final draft was presented to the Congress on June 28, 1776.</p>
<p>Congress debated the Declaration and deleted nearly a fourth of the text, most notably a passage critical of the slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> While Jefferson resented the changes, he did not speak publicly about the revisions. <sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> On July 4, 1776, the Congress ratified the Declaration of Independence and the delegates signed the document on August 2. The Declaration is considered one of Jefferson's major achievements; his preamble is regarded as an enduring statement of human rights. Indeed, the phrase "<a href="/wiki/All_men_are_created_equal" title="All men are created equal">all men are created equal</a>" has been called "one of the best-known sentences in the English language" containing "the most potent and consequential words in American history".<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson viewed the independence of the American people from the mother country <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Britain</a> as breaking away from "parent stock", and that the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">War of Independence</a> from Britain was a natural outcome of being separated by the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hellenbrand_p70_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellenbrand_p70-52"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> In his view, English colonists were compelled to rely on "common sense" and rediscover the "<a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">laws of nature</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Hellenbrand_p70_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellenbrand_p70-52"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> He also maintained the Independence of the original British colonies was part of a historical pattern following a similar divergence when the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> left their mother country Europe hundreds of years earlier and colonized Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Hellenbrand_p70_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellenbrand_p70-52"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_state_legislator_and_Governor">Virginia state legislator and Governor</span></h3>
<p>After the colonies declared their Independence, Jefferson was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> for <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> in September 1776, where the finalization of a state constitution was a priority.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-53"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fer26_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fer26-54"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> He commented on the drafting of the constitution, saying he supported freehold <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a>, by which only landowners could vote. For nearly three years, he assisted with the constitution. He was especially proud of the Bill for Establishing Religious Freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> He served as a Delegate until 1779. Another object of importance to him was to disestablish the Anglican church in Virginia, but this was not done until 1786, while he was in France as US Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Ludwell_Lee" title="Thomas Ludwell Lee">Thomas Ludwell Lee</a> died in 1778 Jefferson was given the task of studying and revising the state's laws. Jefferson drafted 126 bills in three years, including laws to establish fee simple tenure in land and to streamline the judicial system. Jefferson's statutes abolished <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> and provided for general education, which he considered the basis of "republican government."<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-53"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> His "Bill for the More General Diffusion of Knowledge" led to some small changes at the College of William &amp; Mary, and reduced control by clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/220px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/330px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/440px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1520" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Palace_(Williamsburg,_Virginia)" title="Governor's Palace (Williamsburg, Virginia)">Governor's Palace</a> - Governor Jefferson's residence in Williamsburg</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1779, at the age of thirty-six, Jefferson was elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">Governor of Virginia</a> by the two houses of the legislature for a term of one year, then re-elected for an additional year.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> As governor in 1780, he transferred the state capital from Williamsburg to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>. A range of measures were introduced by Jefferson during his time as Governor of Virginia, including public education, inheritance laws and religious freedom. He served as a wartime governor, as the united colonies continued the Revolutionary War against Great Britain, and prepared Richmond in 1780 for an attack, by moving all military supplies to a foundry located five miles outside of town. In January 1781 General <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a> captured the foundry during his invasion of Richmond. Jefferson called for the Virginia militia to defend the city, but when the defense arrived, led by <a href="/wiki/Sampson_Mathews" title="Sampson Mathews">Sampson Mathews</a>, it was too late to prevent the siege.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson evacuated Richmond as the armies engaged.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cornwallis that spring dispatched a 250-man cavalry force led by <a href="/wiki/Banastre_Tarleton" title="Banastre Tarleton">Banastre Tarleton</a> on a secret expedition to capture the Governor and members of the Assembly at Monticello but <a href="/wiki/Jack_Jouett" title="Jack Jouett">Jack Jouett</a> of the Virginia militia thwarted the British plan. Jefferson escaped to <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Forest" title="Poplar Forest">Poplar Forest</a>, his plantation to the west.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> His gubernatorial term expired in June and he spent much of the summer with his family at Poplar Forest. His tenure as governor in general, and his decision to flee the capital in particular, has been criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The members of the General Assembly quickly reconvened in June 1781 in <a href="/wiki/Staunton,_Virginia" title="Staunton, Virginia">Staunton, Virginia</a> across the Blue Ridge Mountains, and considered an official inquiry into Jefferson's actions, as they believed he had failed his responsibilities as governor. Jefferson was not re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-Fer26_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fer26-54"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> In that year also his daughter Lucy died at age one. A second daughter of that name was born the following year but died as well at age 3.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia"><i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></div>
<p>Jefferson received a letter of inquiry in 1780 about the geography, history and government of Virginia from French diplomat <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Barb%C3%A9-Marbois" title="François Barbé-Marbois">François Barbé-Marbois</a>, who was gathering data on the United States. Jefferson included his written responses in a book, <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></i> (1785).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> He compiled the book over five years, including reviews of scientific knowledge, Virginia's history, politics, laws, culture and geography. He was assisted by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walker_(explorer)" title="Thomas Walker (explorer)">Thomas Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Rogers_Clark" title="George Rogers Clark">George R. Clark</a> and geographer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hutchins" title="Thomas Hutchins">Thomas Hutchins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The book is his argument about what constitutes a good society, which he believed was embodied in Virginia. It also included extensive data about the state's natural resources and its economy. He as well wrote extensively about slavery, <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a> and his belief that blacks and whites could not live together as free people in one society because of resentments over slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> He said that, "Those who labor in the earth are the chosen people of God, if ever he had chosen a people."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1785 Jefferson's <i>Notes??'?</i> was anonymously published in Paris in a limited French edition of a few hundred copies. Its first public English edition, issued by John Stockdale in London, appeared in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Congress">Member of Congress</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/220px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/330px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/440px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Independence Hall Assembly Room where Jefferson served in Congress</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following its victory in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">peace treaty</a> with Great Britain in 1783, the United States formed a <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Continental Congress</a> to which Jefferson was appointed as a Virginia delegate. As a member of the committee setting foreign exchange rates, he <a href="/wiki/Plan_for_Establishing_Uniformity_in_the_Coinage,_Weights,_and_Measures_of_the_United_States" title="Plan for Establishing Uniformity in the Coinage, Weights, and Measures of the United States">recommended</a> an American currency based on the decimal system and his plan was adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> He also advised formation of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_States" title="Committee of the States">Committee of the States</a>, to fill the power vacuum when Congress was in recess.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> When Congress adjourned the following June, the Committee met but within two months disagreements divided the members. Jefferson learned of the ordeal while in France, and spoke to Franklin who compared the Committee to a "needed light house" and its members to a "raging sea", rendering it inaccessible and dysfunctional.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1783–84 session of the Congress Jefferson acted as chairman of important committees, to establish a viable system of government for the new Republic, and propose a policy for the settlement of the western territories. Jefferson was the principal author of the <a href="/wiki/Land_Ordinance_of_1784" title="Land Ordinance of 1784">Land Ordinance of 1784</a> whereby Virginia ceded to the national government the vast area it claimed northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. He insisted this territory not be used as colonial territory by any of the thirteen states, but that it be divided into sections which could become states.<sup id="cite_ref-Stewart39_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart39-74"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> He plotted borders for nine new states in their initial stages and also wrote an ordinance banning slavery in all the nation's territories. Congress made extensive revisions including rejection of the ban of slavery. Jefferson thought that Congress had "mutilated" his work, but accepted the majority's changes.<sup id="cite_ref-Peretson189_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-75"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The provisions banning slavery, known later as the <i>Jefferson Proviso</i>, were modified and implemented three years later in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance of 1787</a> and became the law for the entire Northwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Peretson189_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-75"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_to_France">Minister to France</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG/220px-Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG/330px-Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG/440px-Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG 2x" data-file-width="629" data-file-height="840" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_1786_by_Mather_Brown.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of Thomas Jefferson while in London in 1786, by <a href="/wiki/Mather_Brown" title="Mather Brown">Mather Brown</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was sent by the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Confederation Congress</a><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> to join Benjamin Franklin and John Adams as ministers in Europe for negotiation of trade agreements with England, Spain, and France. Some believed the recently widowed Jefferson was depressed and that the assignment would distract him from his wife's death.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Taking his young daughter Patsy and two servants, he departed from Boston in July of 1784 and arrived in Paris the next month.<sup id="cite_ref-Stewart39_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart39-74"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson taught himself to read and write Spanish during the nineteen-day voyage, using a copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">Don Quixote</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Four days after his arrival, Jefferson rode out to <a href="/wiki/Passy" title="Passy">Passy</a> to greet Franklin.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> When the French foreign minister, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gravier,_comte_de_Vergennes" title="Charles Gravier, comte de Vergennes">Count de Vergennes</a>, commented to Jefferson, "You replace Monsieur Franklin, I hear," Jefferson replied, "I <i>succeed</i> him. No man can <i>replace</i> him."<sup id="cite_ref-Hale119_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hale119-82"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Franklin resigned as minister to France in March 1785, and departed in July after a ceremony at Passy.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had Patsy educated at the <a href="/wiki/Pentemont_Abbey" title="Pentemont Abbey">Pentemont Abbey</a>; he taught her French and helped her with her studies. To serve the <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Paris_house_memorial.jpg" title="File:Thomas Jefferson's Paris house memorial.jpg">household</a>, Jefferson brought some of his slaves, including <a href="/wiki/James_Hemings" title="James Hemings">James Hemings</a>, whom he had trained in French cuisine.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1786, Jefferson, through his artist friend <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>, met and fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Maria_Cosway" title="Maria Cosway">Maria Cosway</a>, an accomplished, and married, Italian-English musician of 27. They saw each other frequently over a period of six weeks. She returned to Great Britain, but they maintained a lifelong correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson sent for his youngest surviving child, nine-year-old Polly, in June 1787. She was accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a>, a slave and younger sister of James. That year he suffered a fall and fractured his right wrist, requiring him to write with his left hand, at least for a time.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While in France he became a regular companion of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Lafayette</a>, and used his influence to procure trade agreements with France. Duties on whale oil were removed, and Jefferson directed more of the tobacco trade directly to France eliminating British intermediaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowers328_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowers328-88"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burstein120_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein120-89"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> He often dined with many of the city's prominent people, and accumulated various wines for return to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson corresponded with many pivotal supporters of revolution including the <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Gabriel_Riqueti,_comte_de_Mirabeau" title="Honoré Gabriel Riqueti, comte de Mirabeau">Comte de Mirabeau</a>, a popular pamphleteer who repeated ideals that had been the basis for the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote a letter to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carrington" title="Edward Carrington">Edward Carrington</a> expressing similar concepts he held of the natural tendencies of government and its relationship to the people, saying in one instance, "the natural process of things is for liberty to yield, and government to gain ground."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> began, Jefferson allowed his Paris residence, the Hôtel de Langeac, to be used for meetings by Lafayette and other republicans; he was in Paris during the storming of the Bastille.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson often found his mail opened and inspected by various postmasters, so he began to write his important messages using a code, and invented his own enciphering device, the "Wheel Cipher".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Indeed, he wrote important communications in codes the remainder of his career.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson left Paris in September 1789, he thought temporarily,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> but was prevented from returning by virtue of his appointment as the nation's chief diplomat.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> He remained a firm supporter of the French Revolution, although he was opposed to some of its very violent elements.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State">Secretary of State</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/220px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/330px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thomas Jefferson<br />
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Peale</a>, 1791</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after Jefferson's return from France, he accepted President Washington's invitation to serve as Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> In his new position, Jefferson strongly opposed Treasury Secretary Alexander Hamilton on issues of national fiscal policy, especially the funding of war debts.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> He later associated Hamilton's <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> with "Royalism," and said the "Hamiltonians were panting after&#160;... crowns, coronets and <a href="/wiki/Mitre" title="Mitre">mitres</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first major issues before the Cabinet were the national debt and the permanent location of the capital. Jefferson had always opposed the mounting debt. Hamilton, desirous of the national government consolidating the various states' debts, proposed his <a href="/wiki/Funding_Act_of_1790" title="Funding Act of 1790">Assumption bill</a> to which Jefferson found objection.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> They also differed on the permanent location of the capital. Hamilton wanted the capital close to the major commercial centers of the Northeast, whereas Washington and Jefferson, along with other agrarians, wanted it located to the south. After much deliberation, the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1790" title="Compromise of 1790">Compromise of 1790</a> was struck at a private dinner including Madison. Under the terms of this agreement, the nation's capital was located on the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a>, and the federal government assumed the war debts of all 13 states.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1792 Jefferson was alarmed at the political rivalries taking shape, and wrote to Washington, urging him to run for re-election that year as a unifying influence: "The confidence of the whole nation is centred in you. You being at the helm will be more than an answer to every argument which can be used to alarm and lead the people, in any quarter, into violence or secession."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> He entreated the president to rally the citizenry to a party that would defend democracy against the corrupting influence of banks and monied interests, as espoused by the Federalists. Historians recognize this letter as the earliest delineation of <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> principles.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> In their opposition to Hamilton, Jefferson and other Democratic-Republican organizers favored <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> and local control, and sought to prevent concentration of power in a central government. He continued to work closely with Madison to strengthen the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999102_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999102-107"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The French minister Citizen Genet said in 1793: "Senator Morris and Secretary of the Treasury Hamilton&#160;... had the greatest influence over the President's mind, and that it was only with difficulty that he [Jefferson] counterbalanced their efforts."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson supported France against Britain when they fought in 1793.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> He believed that political success at home depended on victory for the French army in Europe, with reservation as to the brutality of the French Revolution.</p>
<p>During his discussions with <a href="/wiki/George_Hammond_(diplomat)" title="George Hammond (diplomat)">George Hammond</a>, first British Minister to the U.S. beginning in 1791, Jefferson tried unsuccessfully to obtain British agreement, 1) to acknowledge their violation of the Treaty of Paris, 2) to vacate their posts in the Northwest and 3) to compensate the United States for slaves whom the British had freed at the end of the war. In keeping with a passion for his private life, Jefferson resigned the cabinet position in December 1793. He is also said to have considered it an opportune time to bolster his political influence from outside the administration. Future developments indeed bore that out.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>From his base at Monticello, he organized nationwide opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> of 1794, thereby giving his party a bona fide cause to harness in combatting the Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The treaty was designed by Hamilton and supported by Washington; it aimed to reduce tensions and enhance friendly relations as well as trade with Britain. Jefferson warned that it would increase British influence and subvert republicanism, saying, "Thus it is, that Hamilton, Jay, etc., in the boldest act they ever ventured on to undermine the government&#160;... A bolder party stroke was never struck."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The Treaty passed, but he made sure it was not renewed when it expired in 1805. Jefferson continued his pro-French stance; during the violence of the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a> in France, he declined to disavow the revolution because "To back away from France would be to undermine the cause of republicanism in America."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1796_and_Vice_Presidency">Election of 1796 and Vice Presidency</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">United States presidential election, 1796</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democrat-Republican_party" title="Democrat-Republican party" class="mw-redirect">Democrat-Republican party</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="443" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1796 Electoral College Vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the presidential campaign of 1796 Jefferson lost the electoral college vote to Federalist John Adams by 71–68, and he was thereby elected as Vice President. He initially intended to forego the swearing-in ceremony, which he thought monarchical, but relented.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> As the commensurate presiding officer of the Senate, Jefferson assumed a more passive role than Adams had as Vice President. He allowed the Senate to freely conduct debates and confined his participation to issues of procedure which brought him an "honorable and easy" role. He was concerned about the absence of rules as to his authority over the Senate. Years before holding this premier office, Jefferson had studied parliamentary law and procedure for forty years, and had transcribed notes on parliamentary law into a manual which he entitled <i>Parliamentary Pocket Book</i>, making him uniquely qualified to preside in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> He had also served on the committee appointed to draw up the rules of order for the Continental Congress in 1776 and as Vice President, he was able to formulate some Senatorial procedures.</p>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> underway, the Federalists under John Adams rebuilt the military, levied new taxes and enacted the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>. Jefferson believed that these laws were intended to suppress Democratic-Republicans, rather than prosecute enemy aliens, and also thought they were unconstitutional, saying, "The violations of the Constitution, propensities to war, to expense&#160;... which we have lately seen, are becoming evident to the people&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> To rally opposition, he and Madison anonymously wrote the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a> respectively, declaring that the federal government had no right to exercise powers not specifically delegated to it by the states.<sup id="cite_ref-Primary_Documents.2C_Alien_and_Sedition_Acts_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Primary_Documents.2C_Alien_and_Sedition_Acts-117"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Though the resolutions followed the "<a href="/wiki/Interposition" title="Interposition">interposition</a>" approach of Madison, Jefferson advocated <a href="/wiki/Nullification_(U.S._Constitution)" title="Nullification (U.S. Constitution)">nullification</a>. Jefferson's Kentucky draft said: "where powers are assumed which have not been delegated, a nullification of the act is the rightful remedy: that every State has a natural right in cases not within the compact, (casus non fœderis) to nullify of their own authority all assumptions of power by others within their limits.".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In writing the Kentucky Resolutions, Jefferson warned that, "unless arrested at the threshold," the Alien and Sedition Acts would "necessarily drive these states into revolution and blood."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian Chernow has opined that the theoretical damage of the Kentucky and Virginia resolutions was "deep and lasting, and was a recipe for disunion."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Washington was so appalled by them that he told Patrick Henry that if "systematically and pertinaciously pursued", they would "dissolve the union or produce coercion."<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow587_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow587-121"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> The influence of Jefferson's doctrine of states' rights is said to have reverberated to the Civil War and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow551_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow551-122"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He held four confidential talks with the French consul Joseph Letombe in the spring of 1797. In these meetings, Jefferson attacked Adams, predicted that he would only serve one term, and encouraged France to invade England. Jefferson advised Letombe to stall any American envoys sent to Paris by instructing him to "listen to them and then drag out the negotiations at length and mollify them by the urbanity of the proceedings." This toughened the tone that the French government adopted with the new Adams administration. Due to pressure on the Adams administration from Jefferson and his supporters, Congress released the papers related to the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a>, which rallied a shift in popular opinion from Jefferson and the French government to the support of Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow551_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow551-122"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1800">Election of 1800</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="542" data-file-height="446" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1800 Electoral college vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>
<p>The presidential election of 1800 found Jefferson's Democratic-Republican Party garnering the majority of votes in the electoral college; however, Jefferson and fellow party candidate <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> received an equal number of votes. Due to the tie, the election was decided by the Federalist-dominated House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup> Federalist leader <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> convinced his party that Jefferson would be a lesser political evil than Burr.</p>
<p>On February 17, 1801, after thirty-six ballots, the House elected Jefferson President and Burr Vice President.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson is said to have owed his election victory to the South's inflated number of Electors, which counted slaves under the <a href="/wiki/Three-fifths_compromise" title="Three-fifths compromise" class="mw-redirect">three-fifths compromise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-126"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NPR_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-127"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> It was also alleged that Jefferson, through Maryland representative, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Smith_(Maryland)" title="Samuel Smith (Maryland)">Samuel Smith</a>, secured <a href="/wiki/James_Asheton_Bayard_II" title="James Asheton Bayard II" class="mw-redirect">James Asheton Bayard's</a> tie-breaking electoral vote in exchange for guaranteeing the retention of various Federalist posts in the government.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson disputed the allegation and no documents are conclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1801.E2.80.931809">Presidency 1801–1809</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Presidency of Thomas Jefferson">Presidency of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg/220px-ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg/330px-ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg/440px-ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1054" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ThomasJeffersonByRobertField.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of Jefferson by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Field_(painter)" title="Robert Field (painter)">Robert Field</a> (1800)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was sworn in by Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> at the new Capitol in Washington DC on March 4, 1801. In contrast to his predecessors, Jefferson exhibited a dislike of formal etiquette; he arrived alone on horseback without escort and dressed in plain attire.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He was regarded by his supporters as the 'People's President'. Jefferson's election was well received and the event was marked by celebrations throughout the country. Nevertheless, at the time partisan strife between the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic-Republican Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Democratic-Republican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a> parties had grown to new levels. Some of Jefferson's political opponents referred to him as the "Negro President", with critics like the <i>Mercury and New-England Palladium of Boston</i> stating that he had the gall to celebrate his election as a victory for democracy when he won "the temple of Liberty on the shoulders of slaves."<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-127"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Upon assumed office he first confronted an $83 million national debt.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> He began to dismantle Hamilton's Federalist fiscal system. His Secretary of Treasury, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a>, claimed that "if this administration shall not reduce taxes, they never will be permanently reduced."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The Swiss born Gallatin was Jefferson's most valued administrator and a critic of Alexander Hamilton's fiscal policy.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson41_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-133"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson's administration began by eliminating the whiskey excise and all other federal internal taxes, claiming that closing "unnecessary offices", as well as cutting "useless establishments and expenses", allowed for the discontinuation of internal taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> The new executive also attempted to disassemble the national bank and its effect of increasing the national debt. Also reduced was much of the Navy, deemed unnecessary during peacetime.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson nominated moderate Republicans including James Madison as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> Secretary of War, <a href="/wiki/Levi_Lincoln,_Sr." title="Levi Lincoln, Sr.">Levi Lincoln</a> Attorney General, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(Cabinet_member)" title="Robert Smith (Cabinet member)">Robert Smith</a> Secretary of Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson41_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-133"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> At the conclusion of his two terms, he had reduced the national debt by over $12 million.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1801 the Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> announced that the president had appointed Major <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Williams_(engineer)" title="Jonathan Williams (engineer)">Jonathan Williams</a> to investigate the establishment of a national school of military education.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the advice of Washington, Adams, Hamilton and others,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson and the Congress in 1802 authorized the funding and construction of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at West Point on the Hudson River. On March 16, 1802, he signed the Military Peace Establishment Act, directing that a corps of engineers be established and "constitute a Military Academy."
