The Act provided well-trained officers for a professional army. The operation of the US Military Academy at West Point formally started on July 4, 1802.</p>
<p>Jefferson pardoned several of those imprisoned under the Alien and Sedition Acts. He repealed the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges_Act" title="Midnight Judges Act">Judiciary Act of 1801</a>, which removed nearly all of Adams' "midnight judges" from office, and led to the Supreme Court's decision in the landmark case of <i><a href="/wiki/Marbury_v._Madison" title="Marbury v. Madison">Marbury v. Madison</a></i>. Another provision was repealed in the Judiciary Act which had required supreme court justices to travel the country extensively to serve as circuit court judges. Jefferson also signed into law a bill that officially segregated the U.S. postal system by not allowing blacks to carry the mail.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="(January 2014)">why?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Barbary_War">First Barbary War</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First Barbary War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Map. Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/220px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" width="220" height="88" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/330px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/440px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1247" data-file-height="498" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806 - left is Morocco at Gibraltar, center is Tunis, right is Tripoli</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The First Barbary War was declared during Jefferson's initial term as president and was the first conducted by the U.S. on foreign soil and seas.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> American merchant ships had previously been protected from the Barbary pirates by the British navy.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> For decades later, <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">North African</a> pirates captured American merchant ships, pillaged valuable cargoes and enslaved crew members, followed by ransom demands.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes36_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes36-144"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had previously recommended naval forces be limited to those required for coastal defense, but the pirate attacks and the systematic kidnapping of American crew members expanded the need. In 1801 the cabinet voted unanimously to send a fleet of three <a href="/wiki/Frigates" title="Frigates" class="mw-redirect">frigates</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooner</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect">Mediterranean</a> under the command of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dale" title="Richard Dale">Richard Dale</a>, with orders to make a show of force while negotiating for peace if possible; nevertheless, Tripoli declared war upon the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> The American navy forced <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> into breaking their alliance with Tripoli which ultimately left it disarmed. Jefferson also ordered five separate naval bombardments of Tripoli, which temporarily restored peace in the Mediterranean,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana_Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a></div>
<p>In 1802, Jefferson initiated a negotiation for the purchase of the city of New Orleans and adjacent coastal areas from France. The area under discussion mushroomed, and Napoleon I agreed to sell a territory in excess of a million square miles for $15 million, less $3.75 million in previously settled debts owed to the U.S.. Total inhabitants were 80,000–90,000 including about 40,000 slaves. The purchase was ratified by the Senate by a vote of 24–7.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Most thought this was an exceptional opportunity, apart from Constitutional reservations as to the power of the government to acquire land.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy-148"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> The new territory proved to be one of the largest fertile tracts of land on the planet, and it marked the end of French imperial ambitions in North America which conflicted with American expansion west.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis208_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis208-149"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Louisiana Purchase was domestically complicated by the pre-existing establishment of French slaveholders there. Faced with the option to confiscate the slaves of French nationals, Jefferson chose to quickly integrate resident settlers politically and legally into U.S. territories, allowing for slavery to continue in the newly acquired territory along with the local adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Code Napoleon</a>. Since the purchase, historians have differed in their assessments regarding constitutional and slavery issues, but Jefferson's acquisition is considered a pivotal contribution toward America's western growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Malone_DOAB_p21_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone_DOAB_p21-150"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lewis_and_Clark_and_other_expeditions">Lewis and Clark and other expeditions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Red River Expedition (1806)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pike_Expedition" title="Pike Expedition" class="mw-redirect">Pike Expedition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png/220px-Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png/330px-Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png/440px-Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="2880" data-file-height="1800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_Clark_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Louisiana Purchase and the Lewis &amp; Clark Expedition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Anticipating further westward settlements as a result of the Louisiana Purchase, Jefferson arranged for the exploration and mapping of the unchartered territory. It was also important to establish a U.S. claim ahead of competing European interests, and to find the remote <a href="/wiki/Northwest_passage" title="Northwest passage" class="mw-redirect">Northwest passage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose76_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose76-151"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> Knowledge of the western continent was limited to what had been learned casually from trappers and traders.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose.2C_1996_p._76_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose.2C_1996_p._76-152"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Influenced by exploration accounts of Le Page du Pratz in Louisiana (1763) and Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> in the Pacific (1784),<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson and others persuaded Congress in 1804 to fund an expedition to explore and map the newly acquired territory to the Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson appointed <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Clark_(explorer)" title="William Clark (explorer)" class="mw-redirect">William Clark</a> leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Corps_of_Discovery" title="Corps of Discovery">Corps of Discovery</a>, to explore and document scientific and geographic knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Lewis had extensive military woodlands experience and proved an apt student of the sciences of mapping, botany, natural history, mineralogy and navigation.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose.2C_1996_p._76_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose.2C_1996_p._76-152"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Lewis and Clark recruited a company of 45 men and spent a winter preparing near St. Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Setting out in May 1804 and guided by <a href="/wiki/Sacagawea" title="Sacagawea">Sacagawea</a> and various Native-American tribes along the way, the expedition traced the Columbia River and reached the Pacific Ocean by November 1805. They returned to St. Louis September 23, 1806, having lost only one man to disease. The expedition obtained a wealth of scientific and geographic knowledge, including knowledge of the many Indian tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the <i>Corps of Discovery</i>, Jefferson organized three other western expeditions including the <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar_(explorer)" title="William Dunbar (explorer)">William Dunbar</a> and George Hunter expedition on the Ouachita River (1804–1805), the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Thomas Freeman and Peter Custis expedition</a> (1806) on the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pike_Expedition" title="Pike Expedition" class="mw-redirect">Zebulon Pike expedition</a> (1806–1807) into the Rocky Mountains and the Southwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Berry.2C_Beasley.2C_Clements_2006_p._xi_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry.2C_Beasley.2C_Clements_2006_p._xi-158"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> All of the excursions sent out under Jefferson's presidency produced valuable information about the American frontier.<sup id="cite_ref-Berry.2C_Beasley.2C_Clements_2006_p._xi_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry.2C_Beasley.2C_Clements_2006_p._xi-158"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_American_and_Haitian_policies">Native American and Haitian policies</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_Indian_removal" title="Thomas Jefferson and Indian removal">Thomas Jefferson and Indian removal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JEFFERSON,_Thomas-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/220px-JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/330px-JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/440px-JEFFERSON%2C_Thomas-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7240" data-file-height="8542" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JEFFERSON,_Thomas-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> Engraved portrait of Jefferson</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As governor of Virginia during the Revolutionary War, Jefferson had recommended forcibly moving to lands west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> tribes who had allied with the British. But once in the White House, Jefferson wanted to avoid an armed conflict with Native Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> He told his Secretary of War, General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a>, then in charge of Indian affairs: "if we are constrained to lift the hatchet against any tribe, we will never lay it down until that tribe is exterminated, or driven beyond the Mississippi."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> With the colonial and native civilizations in collision, compounded by British incitement of Indian tribes, Jefferson's administration took quick measures to avert another major conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller2008_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2008-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He made a deal with officials of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>: if Georgia would release its legal claims to lands to its west, the U.S. military would help expel the Cherokee people from Georgia. This facilitated his policy of western expansion, to "advance compactly as we multiply".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> His deal arguably violated an existing treaty between the United States government and the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_Nation_(19th_century)" title="Cherokee Nation (19th century)" class="mw-redirect">Cherokee Nation</a>, which guaranteed its people the right to their historic lands.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller2008_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2008-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson believed that natives should abandon their own cultures, religions and lifestyles, and assimilate to western European customs and agriculture, which was considered more beneficial.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller2008_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2008-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> He also contended that integration of Native Americans into the European-American economy would make them more dependent on trade, and they would eventually be willing to give up land in exchange for trade goods or debt curtailments.<sup id="cite_ref-letterharrison1803_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-letterharrison1803-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> In keeping with his trade and acculturation policy, Jefferson kept <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hawkins" title="Benjamin Hawkins">Benjamin Hawkins</a> as Superintendent of Indian Affairs for the Southeastern peoples, who became known as the <a href="/wiki/Five_Civilized_Tribes" title="Five Civilized Tribes">Five Civilized Tribes</a> for their adoption of European-American ways.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> was founded in 1804 as the second republic in the world after its successful slave revolution in the French colony of Saint-Domingue. Jefferson refused to diplomatically recognize Haiti, fearing the success of the "slave republic" would rouse the American South's slaves to rebellion. Jefferson also supported an arms and trade embargo against Haiti.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, during the Haitian revolution, when Jefferson wanted to discourage French control in 1802–1803, he allowed arms and contraband goods to reach Saint-Domingue.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burr.E2.80.93Wilkinson_collusions">Burr–Wilkinson collusions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">Burr–Hamilton duel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burr_conspiracy" title="Burr conspiracy">Burr conspiracy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/240px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg" width="240" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/360px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/480px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="442" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Picture of Burr-Hamilton duel July 11, 1804, from the painting by J. Mund</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Frustrated by Republican Party national dominance, a few New England Federalists in 1804 launched a misguided effort to secede northern states from the Union. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBannner_197434.2C_35_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBannner_197434.2C_35-167"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>, Jefferson's Vice President, ran for governor of New York, and showed interest in the movement; however, Burr lost the election and the New England conspiracy ended. Burr ended his political career when he mortally wounded Hamilton in a duel at <a href="/wiki/Weehawken,_New_Jersey" title="Weehawken, New Jersey">Weehawken</a> that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBannner_197435_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBannner_197435-168"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During 1805 the Jefferson administration was faced with disputes over the exact boundaries of the Louisiana Territory with Mexico, and the fate of the Floridas, which Spain refused to cede to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> Burr in 1806 spread rumors of military adventurism, troop enlistment, increases in arms and ships on the upper Ohio River. Conspiring with Burr in this misinformation was Jefferson appointee Governor <a href="/wiki/James_Wilkinson" title="James Wilkinson">James Wilkinson</a> who acted as a spy for Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanner_197435_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner_197435-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> Burr's plan included the capture of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, invading <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and uniting the Western part of the United States with the conquered country, by an army led by Wilkinson.<sup id="cite_ref-Melton_2002_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melton_2002-172"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Wilkinson inexplicably renounced the plot and reported Burr's treachery to Jefferson from New Orleans. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> In November Jefferson issued a proclamation that persons including "citizens of the United States" were conspiring to take over Spanish territory.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson requested Congressional authority to employ land or naval forces of the U.S. "in cases of insurrection", and in his message to Congress on January 1807, he declared Burr's "guilt is placed beyond question". By late March 1807 Burr was arrested and charged with treason but was acquitted at trial. Wilkinson, who barely escaped indictment, had deleted from court documents evidence of his earlier collaboration with Burr. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson did not appear in person to answer Chief Justice John Marshall's subpoena to testify, but sent relevant documents instead, setting a precedent for executive privilege. Though Burr's acquittal enraged Jefferson, his career was shattered.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson removed Wilkinson as territorial governor but retained him in the U.S. military. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner_197437-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reelection_in_1804">Reelection in 1804</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">United States presidential election, 1804</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1804 Electoral college vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson's successful first term occasioned his re-nomination for president by the Republican party and his re-election in 1804 for a second term.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a> replaced Burr as his running mate following Burr's killing of Hamilton in their <a href="/wiki/Burr-Hamilton_duel" title="Burr-Hamilton duel" class="mw-redirect">duel</a> of July that year. The Federalist party ran <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a> of South Carolina, who had been John Adam's vice presidential candidate. The Jefferson-Clinton ticket won overwhelmingly in the electoral college vote, by 162 to 14, promoting their achievement of lower taxes, booming economic prosperity and the Louisiana Purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A split developed in the Republican party, led by fellow Virginian <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph of Roanoke</a> in March 1806. Jefferson and Madison had backed resolutions to limit or ban British imports in retaliation for British actions against American shipping. Madison also proposed spending $20 million in roads and canals in infrastructure, leading to the National Road west from Maryland. Randolph felt these measures were akin to Federalist activism, and he formed a congressional caucus of "<a href="/wiki/Tertium_quids" title="Tertium quids">Quids</a>", calling for purity in republican principles and roundly denouncing both Jefferson and Madison.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's popularity further suffered in his second term due to his response to wars in Europe. Positive relations with Great Britain had diminished due partly to the antipathy between Jefferson and the British Ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Merry" title="Anthony Merry">Anthony Merry</a>. And after Napoleon's decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Battle of Austerlitz</a> in 1805, Napoleon became more aggressive in his negotiations over trading rights, which American efforts failed to counter. Jefferson then led the enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, directed at both France and Great Britain. This triggered economic chaos in the US and was strongly criticized at the time, resulting in Jefferson having to abandon the policy a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following the Revolution the states abolished the international slave trade, but South Carolina reopened it. Jefferson in his annual message of December 1806 denounced the "violations of human rights" attending the international slave trade, calling on the newly elected Congress to criminalize it immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1807, congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a>, which Jefferson signed.<sup id="cite_ref-miller145_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller145-180"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randal583_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randal583-181"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> While the act established severe punishment against international slave trade, it did not address the issue domestically.</p>
<p>In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>, Jefferson sought to annex Florida from Spain, as brokered by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress agreed to the president's request to secretly appropriate purchase money, in the "$2,000,000 Bill".<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> The Congressional funding drew criticism from Randolph who believed the money would wind up in coffers of Napoleon. The bill was signed into law; however, negotiations for the project failed. Jefferson lost clout among fellow Republicans and his use of unofficial Congressional channels was sharply criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chesapeake.E2.80.93Leopard_Affair_and_Embargo_Act">Chesapeake–Leopard Affair and Embargo Act</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake%E2%80%93Leopard_Affair" title="Chesapeake–Leopard Affair">Chesapeake–Leopard Affair</a></div>
<p>The British conducted raids on American shipping and kidnapped seamen in 1806–07; thousands of Americans were thus <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressed</a> into their service. In 1806 Jefferson issued a call for a boycott of British goods; on April 18 Congress passed the Non-Importation Acts but they were never enforced. Later that year Jefferson asked James Monroe and <a href="/wiki/William_Pinkney" title="William Pinkney">William Pinkney</a> to negotiate with Great Britain to end the harassment of American shipping, though Britain showed no signs of improving relations. A treaty was finalized, however it lacked any provisions to end impressment.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes505_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes505-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The British ship <i>HMS Leopard</i> fired upon the <a href="/wiki/USS_Chesapeake_(1799)" title="USS Chesapeake (1799)">USS <i>Chesapeake</i></a> off the Virginia coast in June 1807, and Jefferson prepared for war.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> He issued a proclamation banning armed British ships from U.S. waters. He presumed unilateral authority to call on the states to prepare 100,000 militia and ordered the purchase of arms, ammunition and supplies, saying "The laws of necessity, of self-preservation, of saving our country when in danger, are of higher obligation [than strict observance of written laws]". The <a href="/wiki/USS_Revenge_(1806)" title="USS Revenge (1806)">USS Revenge</a>, dispatched to demand an explanation from the British government, was also fired upon. Jefferson called for a special session of Congress in October to enact an embargo or in the alternative consider war.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/220px-Ograbme.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/330px-Ograbme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/440px-Ograbme.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon showing merchants dodging the "Ograbme", which is 'Embargo' spelled backwards (1807)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December news arrived that Napoleon had extended the Berlin Decree, globally banning British imports. In Britain, George III ordered redoubling efforts at impressment, including American sailors. But the war fever of the summer faded–Congress had no appetite to prepare the U.S. for war. Jefferson asked for and received the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act</a>, the optimal choice for him, as it gained more time for building up defensive works, militias and naval forces. Historian Jon Meecham opined the Embargo Act was a projection of power which surpassed the Alien and Sedition Acts.</p>
<p>Secretary of State James Madison supported the embargo with more vigor than Jefferson, while the Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> opposed, due to its indefinite time frame and the risk it posed to the policy of American neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-adams_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adams-186"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> The U.S. economy suffered and criticism grew, with Jefferson's party losing support, and efforts began to work around the embargo. Instead of retreating, Jefferson sent federal agents to secretly track down smugglers and violators.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> Three acts were passed in Congress during 1807 and 1808, called the <i>Supplementary</i>, the <i>Additional</i> and the <i>Enforcement</i> acts.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> Though the government could not prevent American vessels from trading with the European belligerents once they had left American ports, the embargo triggered a devastating decline in exports. Shortly before leaving office in March 1809, Jefferson signed the repeal of the Embargo. In its place the <a href="/wiki/Non-Intercourse_Act" title="Non-Intercourse Act" class="mw-redirect">Non-Intercourse Act</a> was passed, but it proved no more effective.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson believed that the failure of the embargo was due to selfish traders and merchants showing a lack of "republican virtue" by not complying with it.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes505_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes505-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Many historians have considered that Jefferson's embargo was ineffective and harmful to American interests,<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> Others portray it as an innovative non violent measure which aided France in its war with Britain, while preserving American neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes505_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes505-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson maintained that, had the embargo been widely observed, it would have avoided war in 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> – March 1, 1803</li>
</ul>
<p>As president, Jefferson used his influence to bring Ohio into the Union on April 30, 1802, the first state under the Northwest Ordinance. In Congress, Jefferson had authored the Ordinance of 1787 in Congressional committee under the Articles of Confederation. He was therefore instrumental in prohibiting slavery not only to new territories, but in new states beginning with Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Philosophical_Society">American Philosophical Society</span></h2>
<p>Jefferson was for 35 years a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>, founded in 1743 by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, Through the Society he advanced the sciences and Enlightenment ideals, emphasizing that knowledge of science reinforced and extended freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes432_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes432-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> He was elected to the Society in January 1780 while Governor of Virginia and the following year was elected a Counsellor. During his long tenure he served on many committees. He became the Society's third President on March 3, 1797, only days after he was elected Vice President under Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-JeffAPS_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JeffAPS-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Berstein118_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berstein118-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon his acceptance Jefferson stated: "I feel no qualification for this distinguished post but a sincere zeal for all the objects of our institution and an ardent desire to see knowledge so disseminated through the mass of mankind that it may at length reach even the extremes of society, beggars and kings."<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes432_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes432-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time he was compiling data for his <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></i> which he later shared with the Society. He served as the Society's president for the next eighteen years through both terms of his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-JeffAPS_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JeffAPS-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Along with topics on science and discovery, he often discussed ideas of abolition with dedicated abolitionist Society members including <a href="/wiki/Comte_de_Volney" title="Comte de Volney" class="mw-redirect">Comte de Volney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Kosciuszko" title="Tadeusz Kosciuszko" class="mw-redirect">Tadeusz Kosciuszko</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nash142_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nash142-196"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson also introduced <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> to the Society where various scientists tutored him in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JeffAPS_194-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JeffAPS-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose.2C_1996.2C_p._126_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose.2C_1996.2C_p._126-198"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> He offered his letter of resignation on three separate occasions, with the Society refusing each time. The Society finally accepted his resignation at the meeting of January 20, 1815 "with great reluctance". He remained active through correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> After Jefferson's death in 1826 the Society draped the chair he had occupied in black for six months.<sup id="cite_ref-JeffAPS_194-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JeffAPS-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson was also elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-200"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> He became an associated member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences">Royal Institute of the Netherlands</a> in 1809,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> and was a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h2>
<p>In the years following Jefferson's political retirement he spent most of his time pursuing educational interests–selling his vast collection of books to the Library of Congress, and founding and building the University of Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As he settled into private life at Monticello, Jefferson developed a daily routine of rising early, once remarking, "Whether I retire to bed early or late, I rise with the sun." He would spend several hours writing letters, with which he was often deluged. In the midday, he would often inspect the plantation on horseback, visiting the fields and the nailery. In the evenings, his family ate dinner and often recreated in the gardens; late at night, Jefferson would retire to bed with a book. He once wrote, "I never go to bed without an hour, or half hour's previous reading of something moral, whereon to ruminate in the intervals of sleep."<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Virginia">University of Virginia</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;"><a href="/wiki/File:University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Winter landscape of the Rotunda at the University of Virginia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg/236px-University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg" width="236" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg/354px-University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg/472px-University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2902" data-file-height="1906" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rotunda, University of Virginia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>He wanted to found a new institution of higher learning free of church influences where students could specialize in many new areas not offered at other universities. Jefferson believed educating people was a effective way to establish an stable society and that schools should be paid for by the general public in order to be accessible to students from all social strata.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1800, Jefferson wrote a letter to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> about his proposed University.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1819, the 76-year-old Jefferson founded the University of Virginia. He initiated and organized the legislative campaign for its charter and with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Bacon_(1785%E2%80%931866)" title="Edmund Bacon (1785–1866)">Edmund Bacon</a>, purchased the location. Jefferson was the principal designer of the buildings. He also planned the University's curriculum and served as the first rector. Upon opening in 1825, it was the first university to offer a full slate of elective courses to its students. With no campus chapel included in the original plans, the university was notable for being centered about a library rather than a church, reinforcing the principle of separation of church and state.</p>
<p>Stylistically, Jefferson was a proponent of the Greek and Roman styles, which he believed to be most representative of American democracy by historical association. Each academic unit was designed with a two-story temple front facing the quadrangle, while the library was modeled on the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Roman Pantheon</a>. In his vision, any citizen of the state could attend school with the sole criterion being ability.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's educational ideas were expressed in the configuration of his campus plan, which he called the "<a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">Academical Village</a>". Individual academic units were defined as distinct structures, each housed classrooms, faculty offices, and residences. Gardens and vegetable plots are placed behind and surrounded by serpentine walls, affirming the importance of the agrarian lifestyle.</p>
<p>After Jefferson died in 1826, James Madison replaced him as the University Rector.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> In a codicil to his last will, Jefferson left most of his library to the University.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> Until his death, Jefferson invited students and faculty of the college to his home.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lafayette.27s_visit">Lafayette's visit</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_the_Marquis_de_Lafayette_to_the_United_States" title="Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States">Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States</a></div>
<p>Jefferson learned in the summer of 1824 that <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Lafayette</a> had accepted an invitation from President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> to visit the United States. Lafayette arrived at Monticello on November 4 in a carriage provided by Jefferson with a military escort of 120 men. Some 200 friends and neighbors also arrived for the reunion. Jefferson's grandson Randolph was present and witnessed the historic reunion: "As they approached each other, their uncertain gait quickened itself into a shuffling run, and exclaiming, 'Ah Jefferson!' 'Ah Lafayette!', they burst into tears as they fell into each other's arms." Everyone in attendance stood in respectful silence, many of them stifling sobs of their own. Jefferson and Lafayette then retired to the privacy of the house and began reminiscing the many events and encounters they shared years before.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The next morning Jefferson, Lafayette and James Madison rode to the Central Hotel in Charlottesville. After being greeted and honored with speeches they departed the hotel at noon and set out for a tour and banquet at the University of Virginia. In the rotunda of the university with Jefferson seated between Lafayette and Madison they had dinner, with French wine from Monticello. It was the first public function at the University. Jefferson had someone read a speech he had prepared, as his voice was weak. This was Jefferson's last public statement. Lafayette later accepted Jefferson's invitation for honorary membership to the University's <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Literary_and_Debating_Society" title="Jefferson Literary and Debating Society">Jefferson Literary and Debating Society</a>. After an eleven-day visit Lafayette departed Monticello on November 15.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_days">Final days</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Obelisk at Thomas Jefferson's gravesite" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/220px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/330px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/440px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's gravesite</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson's health began to deteriorate in July 1825 from a combination of toxemia, uremia, and pneumonia and by June he was confined to bed. He spent most of his waking hours going over his finances and debts. On May 22 he made his last entry in the 'Farm Book', noting the price of lamp oil and the cost of lighting his estate. On June 24 he wrote his last letter, to Roger Weightman of a Washington newspaper, the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Intelligencer" title="National Intelligencer">National Intelligencer</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> in which he reaffirmed his principles in the Declaration of Independence. On July 3 Jefferson was overcome by fever and declined an invitation to Washington to attend an anniversary celebration of the Declaration.</p>
<p>During the last hours of his life he was accompanied by his grandson <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Thomas Jefferson Randolph">Thomas Jefferson Randolph</a> and his doctor, <a href="/wiki/Robley_Dunglison" title="Robley Dunglison">Robley Dunglison</a>, and other family members and friends, and was at ease with the immediacy of death. He said to Dunglison, "Well Doctor, you see I am still here yet." To the family's words of hope Jefferson impatiently replied, "Do not imagine for a moment that I feel the smallest solicitude as to the result," at which point he gave directions for his funeral, requesting no sort of celebration. He declared, "I have done for my country, and for all mankind, all that I could do, and I now resign my soul, without fear, to my God, – my daughter to my country."<sup id="cite_ref-rayner428.E2.80.9329_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rayner428.E2.80.9329-218"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> After falling back asleep, Jefferson later woke at eight o'clock that evening with a final inquiry, "Is it the fourth yet?" His doctor replied, "It soon will be."<sup id="cite_ref-Berstein189_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berstein189-219"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> On July 4 at 12:50&#160;p.m., Jefferson died at age 83; it was the fiftieth anniversary of the Declaration of Independence and just a few hours before John Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-rayner428.E2.80.9329_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rayner428.E2.80.9329-218"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Berstein189_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berstein189-219"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's funeral, held July 5 and performed by Reverend Charles Clay, was a simple affair. No invitations were sent, but some friends and visitors came to the ceremony and burial to pay their respects. Jefferson's remains were carried by "servants, family and friends" to the family grave site at Monticello.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote his own epitaph, which reads, "HERE WAS BURIED THOMAS JEFFERSON, AUTHOR OF THE DECLARATION OF AMERICAN INDEPENDENCE, OF THE STATUTE OF VIRGINIA FOR RELIGIOUS FREEDOM, AND FATHER OF THE UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA."</p>
<p>Though born into a wealthy family, Jefferson died deeply in debt, unable to pass on his estate freely to his heirs.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> Though he gave instructions for disposal of his assets in his will,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> his estate, possessions, and slaves were sold at public auctions starting in 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1831 Monticello was sold by Martha Jefferson Randolph and the other heirs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation">Historical reputation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_Memorial_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Jefferson_Memorial_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="649" data-file-height="406" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_Memorial_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson Memorial, Washington, DC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson is a historical icon of individual liberty, democracy and republicanism. Some have hailed him as among the most effective architects of the American Revolution, and as a renaissance man who promoted science and scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> Abraham Lincoln called Jefferson "the most distinguished politician in our history."<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson is widely championed as the primary author of the Declaration of Independence, and for having written more than 18,000 letters of political and philosophical substance during his life.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historians have also noted the discrepancy between his articulated views of slavery and his slaveholding, a controversial tenure as governor of Virginia, arguable disloyalty under Washington and Adams, his <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">advocacy of nullification and secession</a>, some personal spending excesses and his diminished second term presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> Some historians have also criticized the harsh treatment of Native Americans during his tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Yet others have noted Jefferson was predominantly a kind and generous employer and master, who expressed deep moral convictions against slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> A number maintain that many of the criticisms leveled at Jefferson overlook much and are politically and racially biased.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_philosophy_and_views">Political philosophy and views</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">Republicanism in the United States</a></div>
<p>Jefferson's political ideals were greatly influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> whom he considered the three greatest men that ever lived.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Gibbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_St_John,_1st_Viscount_Bolingbroke" title="Henry St John, 1st Viscount Bolingbroke">Bolingbroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> He thought the independent <a href="/wiki/Yeoman#United_States" title="Yeoman">yeoman</a> and agrarian life were ideals of republican virtues. He was distrusting of cities and financiers, favored decentralized government power, and believed that the tyranny that had plagued the common man in Europe was due to corrupt political establishments and monarchies. Having supported efforts to disestablish the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> and having authored the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>, he pressed for a <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">wall of separation between church and state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republicans under Jefferson were strongly influenced by the 18th-century British <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" title="Whig (British political party)" class="mw-redirect">Whig Party</a>, who believed in limited government.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> became dominant in <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">early American politics</a> and his views became known as <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_government">Society and government</span></h3>
<p>According to Jefferson, citizens have "certain inalienable rights" and "Rightful liberty is unobstructed action according to our will within limits drawn around us by the equal rights of others&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> Jeffersonian government not only prohibited individuals in society from infringing on the liberty of others, but also restrained itself from diminishing individual liberty as a protection against tyranny from the majority.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> While he believed most people could not escape corrupting dependence, the franchise should be extended only to those who could, including the yeoman farmer. He disliked inter-generational dependence, such as national debt and unalterable governments.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> He was convinced that individual liberties were the fruit of equality, threatened only by government.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> Excesses of democracy in his view were caused by institutional corruptions rather than human nature. He remained less suspicious of working democracy than many of his contemporaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As president, Jefferson tried to re-create the balance between the state and federal governments as it existed under the Articles of Confederation, seeking to shift the balance of power back to the states. He acted out of his republican theory that liberty could only be retained in small, homogeneous societies. He felt that the Federalist system enacted by Washington and Adams had encouraged corrupting patronage and dependence.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of Jefferson's contradictions can be understood within this philosophical framework. For example, he opposed women's right to vote or any participation in politics because a government must be controlled by the economically independent. Instead he argued: "our good ladies&#160;... are contented to soothe and calm the minds of their husbands returning ruffled from political debate."<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democracy">Democracy</span></h3>
<p>As a proponent of democracy, Jefferson considered it to be the very expression of society as a whole, and called for national self-determination, cultural uniformity and education of all males of the commonwealth.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> In his view, public education and a free press were essential to a democratic nation: "If a nation expects to be ignorant and free it expects what never was and never will be.&#160;... The people cannot be safe without information. Where the press is free, and every man able to read, all is safe".<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After leaving Washington's cabinet as Secretary of State, by October 1795 Jefferson's thoughts turned to the electoral bases of the Republicans and Federalists. The "Republican" classification of the United States for which he advocated included 1) "the entire body of landholders" everywhere, and 2) "the body of laborers" without land.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> Republicans united behind Jefferson as Vice President, with the election of 1796 expanding democracy nationwide, including local committees and correspondence networks. County committees framed local Republican tickets and spawned partisan Republican newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> Privately, Jefferson promoted Republican candidates to run for local state offices.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> He sought an aristocracy of merit, an example being his vice presidential candidate choice in George Clinton, the child of Irish immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham405_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-250"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning with Jefferson's electioneering for the "revolution of 1800", his democratic efforts were based on egalitarian appeals.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson in his later years referred to the 1800 election "as real a revolution in the principles of our government as that of '76 was in its form", one "not effected indeed by the sword&#160;... but by the&#160;... suffrage of the people."<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> Voter participation grew in Jefferson's two terms, increasing to "unimaginable levels" compared to the Federalist Era, with doubled turnouts.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup> John Quincy Adams noted following Jefferson's 1804 election, "The power of the Administration rests upon the support of a much stronger majority of the people throughout the Union than the former Administrations ever possessed."<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham405_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-250"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In retirement, Jefferson gradually became critical of his home state for violating "the principle of equal political rights"–the social right of universal male suffrage.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup> Initially, with the onset of the Revolution, Jefferson had accepted Blackstone's principle that property ownership would lead to the independent will required from voters in a republic, but he sought to further expand suffrage by land distribution to the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup> In the heat of the Revolutionary Era and afterward, with Jefferson's support, several states expanded voter eligibility from landed gentry to include male <a href="/wiki/European_Americans" title="European Americans" class="mw-redirect">Euro-American</a> tax-paying citizens, those owning either their own houses or their own tools and paying taxes on them.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> In response to a pamphlet advocating a Virginia Constitutional Convention, he went further than the radical convention promoters. He sought a "general suffrage" of all taxpayers and militia-men, as well as equal representation by voter population in the state legislature, not skewed to favor slave-holding regions of the state. He also favored a reform of Virginia's county courthouse system, to more nearly resemble that of the more democratic townships of New England.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banks">Banks</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg/220px-FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg/330px-FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg/440px-FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2370" data-file-height="1509" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstBankofUS00_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First Bank of U.S., Philadelphia, 1791–1811</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson distrusted government banks, and opposed public borrowing which he thought created long-term debt, bred monopolies and invited dangerous speculation as opposed to productive labor.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup> He argued that each generation should curtail all debt within 19 years, and not impose a long-term debt on subsequent generations.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260"><span>[</span>254<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1791, President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> asked Jefferson, then <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a>, if the Congress had the authority to create a <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">national bank</a>. While Hamilton believed Congress had the authority, Jefferson thought a national bank would ignore the needs of individuals and farmers. He also was convinced it would assume powers not granted to the federal government by the States, and therefore would violate the <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Tenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Tenth Amendment</a> as well as the laws of <i>Mortmain, Alienage, Forfeiture, Distribution, and Monopolies</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261"><span>[</span>255<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262"><span>[</span>256<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263"><span>[</span>257<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson used the agrarian resistance to banks and speculators as the first defining principle of an opposition party, recruiting candidates for congress on the issue as early as 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> In retirement, Jefferson reminded John Taylor in 1816 that they had ever hated the banks which could destroy the state constitutions, already suffering from speculators who "sweep away the fortunes and morals of our citizens". He thought banks were more dangerous than standing armies–"swindling futurity on a large scale".<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span>[</span>260<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits_of_rebellion">Benefits of rebellion</span></h3>
<p>In a letter to James Madison in 1787, Jefferson wrote, "A little rebellion, now and then, is a good thing, and as necessary in the political world as storms in the physical.&#160;... It is a medicine necessary for the sound health of government."<sup id="cite_ref-Melton_2004_277_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melton_2004_277-267"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup> Similarly, in a letter to Abigail Adams on February 22, 1787 he wrote, "The spirit of resistance to government is so valuable on certain occasions that I wish it to be always kept alive. It will often be exercised when wrong, but better so than not to be exercised at all."<sup id="cite_ref-Melton_2004_277_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melton_2004_277-267"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup> Concerning <a href="/wiki/Shays%27_Rebellion" title="Shays' Rebellion">Shays' Rebellion</a> after he had heard of the bloodshed, Jefferson wrote to <a href="/wiki/William_Stephens_Smith" title="William Stephens Smith">William S. Smith</a>, John Adams' son-in-law, "What signify a few lives lost in a century or two? The tree of liberty must from time to time be refreshed with the blood of patriots and tyrants. It is its natural manure."<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> In another letter to Smith that year Jefferson wrote: "And what country can preserve its liberties, if the rulers are not warned from time to time, that this people preserve the spirit of resistance? Let them take arms."<sup id="cite_ref-Melton_2004_277_267-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melton_2004_277-267"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As late as 1804 before his second term began, Jefferson even seemed at ease with the prospect of dividing the nation into separate democracies. In view of a prospective republic in the Mississippi River Valley, they would be "as much our children and our descendants" alongside any coastal confederacy remaining. "I feel myself as much identified with that [western] country&#160;... as with this [United States].<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He said of the French revolution, "The liberty of the whole world was depending on the issue of the contest".<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson once argued that America would become the world's great "empire of liberty"—that is, the model for democracy and republicanism.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272"><span>[</span>265<span>]</span></a></sup> On departing the presidency in 1809, he described America as, "...&#160;trusted with the destinies of this solitary republic of the world, the only monument of human rights, and the sole depository of the sacred fire of freedom and self-government, from hence it is to be lighted up in other regions of the earth, if other regions of the earth shall ever become susceptible of its benign influence."<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span>[</span>266<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_slavery" title="Thomas Jefferson and slavery">Thomas Jefferson and slavery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_slaves.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg/220px-Jefferson_slaves.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg/330px-Jefferson_slaves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_slaves.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's 1795 Farm book, page 30, lists 163 slaves at Monticello.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson lived in the Virginia planter economy largely dependent upon <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>. As a wealthy slave owner, he used slave labor to run his household, plantation, tobacco fields and various shops. The first record Jefferson made in regard to slave ownership was in 1774, when he owned 41.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274"><span>[</span>267<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275"><span>[</span>268<span>]</span></a></sup> In his lifetime he owned about 600 slaves, buying and selling them for the management of his affairs, and regularly maintaining about 130 .<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276"><span>[</span>269<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FinkelmanMyth_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinkelmanMyth-277"><span>[</span>270<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson purchased slaves on occasion in order to unite their families.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He felt slavery was harmful to both slave and master while utilizing many slaves for profit.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup> Historians are divided on whether he opposed the institution, largely because he was silent on emancipation during his presidency and only freed a few slaves on his Monticello plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-EOAAH_p53_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOAAH_p53-282"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> Some researchers suggest Jefferson's slave ownership contrasted with his philosophy of "all men are created equal".<sup id="cite_ref-EOAAH_p53_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOAAH_p53-282"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup> Other historians, however, maintain that the sentiment in this statement is what actually inspired Jefferson to advance legislation to abolish slavery and that he believed slavery was "contrary to the laws of nature" where everyone had a right to personal liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span>[</span>277<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-treatment_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treatment-285"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1790s, Jefferson opened his own mechanized nailery which enabled mostly younger slaves to make nails. However, while machinery in his shop allowed Jefferson's slaves to work more efficiently, he failed to convince southern farmers that machinery could displace slavery. Instead of eliminating the need for slaves, he realized just how dependent on slave labor the south was.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodin.2C_Stephen_B_2006_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodin.2C_Stephen_B_2006-286"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson wrote in 1816 that "where the disease is most deeply seated, there it will be slowest in eradication. In the Northern States it was merely super?cial, and easily corrected.
